Pi..........................................................................................................28\nReference Sheets............................................................................................................29\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n1\nForeword\nThe information contained in this Educator Guide is designed to raise educator awareness of the structure of\nthe New York State Grades 3–8 Mathematics Tests measuring the New York State Next Generation \nMathematics Learning Standards (https://www.nysed.gov/standards-instruction/mathematics).\nThe guide provides educators with pertinent information about the test development process, the learning \nstandards that the tests are designed to measure, the format of the testing sessions, which includes what \ntypes of questions will be asked, the average length of the testing sessions, and what mathematics tools \nare allowable during testing. Links to additional resources are provided to further enhance educators’ \nunderstanding of the structure of the Mathematics tests. Educators are encouraged to review the guide prior \nto the test administration to gain familiarity with the test format. The information presented can also be used \nas a platform for educator discussion on how student assessment results can guide future instruction.\nThe Elementary and Intermediate testing schedule for the spring administration can be found on \nthe Department’s website (https://www.nysed.gov/state-assessment/grades-3-8-test-schedules). Questions \nregarding the New York State Testing Program and test design may be addressed to the Office of State \nAssessment at emscassessinfo@nysed.gov. Questions regarding the New York State Learning Standards \nmay be addressed to the Office of Standards and Instruction at P12StandardsInstruction@nysed.gov.\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n2\nNew York State Grades 3–8 Testing Program\nPurpose of State Testing\nThe federal Every Student Succeeds Act (2015) requires that states annually administer tests in English \nLanguage Arts (ELA) and Mathematics in Grades 3–8. The Grades 3–8 ELA and Mathematics NYS Testing \nProgram has been designed to measure student knowledge and skills as defined by the grade-level New \nYork Next Generation Learning Standards (NGLS) in ELA and Mathematics. The tests are designed to \nreport student proficiency in one of four performance levels. Please refer to page 5 of this guide for further \ninformation regarding the Performance Level Descriptions.\nNew York State Educators Involvement in Test Development\nWhile teachers have always been included in the Grades 3–8 test development process, the New York State \nEducation Department (NYSED) continues to expand the number of opportunities for New York State \neducators to become involved. This includes participating in the development of all the test questions. New \nYork State educators provide the critical input necessary to ensure that the tests are fair, valid, and appropriate \nfor students through their participation in many test development activities.\nThe test development process includes the development, review, and approval of test questions, construction \nof operational test forms, final approval of test forms prior to administration, and the development of \nscoring materials. NYSED remains committed to improving the quality of the State’s assessments and \nthe experiences that students have taking these tests. For more information on opportunities to participate \nin the test development process, please visit the Test Development Participation Opportunities website\n(https://www.nysed.gov/state-assessment/test-development-participation-opportunities).\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n3\nThe Next Generation Mathematics Learning Standards\nThe New York State Next Generation Mathematics Learning Standards define the knowledge, skills, and \nunderstandings that individuals can and do habitually demonstrate over time when exposed to high-quality \ninstructional environments and learning experiences. The Learning Standards, defined through the integration \nof the Standards for Mathematical Content and the Standards for Mathematical Practice, collectively, are \nfocused and cohesive—designed to support student access to the knowledge and understanding of the \nmathematical concepts that are necessary to function in a world very dependent upon the application of \nmathematics. Students are expected to understand math conceptually, use procedural skills, and solve math \nproblems rooted in the real world, deciding for themselves which strategies, formulas, and grade-appropriate \ntools (e.g., ruler, protractor, or calculator) to use.\nStandards for Mathematical Practice\nThe Learning Standards for each grade level (and high school course) begin with the eight Standards for \nMathematical Practice. The Standards for Mathematical Practice describe the ways in which developing \npractitioners increasingly should engage with the subject matter as they grow in mathematical maturity and \nexpertise throughout the elementary, middle, and high school years. References to the integration of the \nStandards for Mathematical Content and the Standards for Mathematical Practice are provided throughout the \nNext Generation Mathematics Learning Standards document (https://www.nysed.gov/standards-instruction/\nmathematics).\nPlease note that the following grade-level overviews do not include every standard/topic that should \nbe included in instruction. Further information about the entire scope of the learning expectations for each \ngrade level, as well as additional instructional considerations that include the within-grade connections, \ngrade-level fluencies, and connecting the Standards for Mathematical Practice to Mathematical Content \ncan be found in the Next Generation Mathematics Learning Standards document and the associated grade\u0002level crosswalks/snapshots located on the NYSED website (https://www.nysed.gov/standards-instruction/\nmathematics).\nGrade 3\nIn Grade 3, instructional time focuses on four areas: (1) developing understanding of multiplication and \ndivision and strategies for multiplication and division within 100; (2) developing understanding of fractions, \nespecially unit fractions (fractions with numerator 1); (3) developing understanding of the structure of \nrectangular arrays and of area; and (4) describing and analyzing polygons based on the number of sides and \nvertices.\nGrade 4\nIn Grade 4, instructional time focuses on three areas: (1) developing understanding and fluency with multi\u0002digit multiplication, and developing understanding of dividing to find quotients involving multi-digit \ndividends; (2) developing an understanding of fraction equivalence, addition and subtraction of fractions with \nlike denominators, and multiplication of fractions by whole numbers; and (3) understanding that geometric \nfigures can be analyzed and classified based on their properties, such as having parallel sides, perpendicular \nsides, particular angle measures, and symmetry.\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n4\nGrade 5\nIn Grade 5, instructional time focuses on three areas: (1) developing fluency with addition and subtraction of \nfractions, and developing understanding of multiplication of fractions and of division of fractions in limited \ncases (unit fractions divided by whole numbers and whole numbers divided by unit fractions); (2) extending \ndivision to 2-digit divisors, integrating decimal fractions into the place value system and developing \nunderstanding of operations with decimals to hundredths, and developing fluency with whole number and \ndecimal operations; and (3) developing understanding of volume.\nGrade 6\nIn Grade 6, instructional time focuses on five areas: (1) connecting ratio and rate to whole number \nmultiplication and division and using concepts of ratio and rate to solve problems; (2) completing \nunderstanding of division of fractions and extending the notion of number to the system of rational numbers, \nwhich includes negative numbers; (3) writing, interpreting, and using expressions and equations; (4) \ndeepening understanding of area, surface area, and volume; and (5) developing understanding of simple \nprobabilities and statistical thinking.\nGrade 7\nIn Grade 7, instructional time focuses on three areas: (1) developing understanding of and applying \nproportional relationships; (2) developing understanding of operations with rational numbers and working \nwith expressions and linear equations; and (3) drawing inferences about populations based on samples.\nGrade 8\nIn Grade 8, instructional time focuses on three areas: (1) formulating and reasoning about expressions and \nequations, including modeling an association in bivariate data with a linear equation, and solving linear \nequations and systems of linear equations; (2) grasping the concept of a function and using functions to \ndescribe quantitative relationships; and (3) analyzing two- and three-dimensional space and figures using \ndistance, angle, similarity, and congruence, and understanding and applying the Pythagorean Theorem.\nFor more information about the Next Generation Mathematics Learning Standards, please refer to \nthe NYSED website (https://www.nysed.gov/standards-instruction/mathematics).\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n5\nPerformance Level Definitions\nFor each subject area, students perform along a continuum of the knowledge and skills necessary to meet \nthe demands of the Learning Standards for English Language Arts and Mathematics. New York State \nassessments are designed to classify student performance into one of four levels based on the knowledge \nand skills the student has demonstrated. Due to the need to identify student proficiency, the state tests must \nprovide students at each performance level opportunities to demonstrate their knowledge and skills in the \nNext Generation Learning Standards. For this reason, the Performance Level Descriptions play a central role \nin the test development process, specifically question writing.\nThese performance levels are defined as:\nNYS Level 4\nStudents performing at this level excel in standards for their grade. They demonstrate knowledge, skills, and \npractices embodied by the Learning Standards that are considered more than sufficient for the expectations \nat this grade.\nNYS Level 3\nStudents performing at this level are proficient in standards for their grade. They demonstrate knowledge, \nskills, and practices embodied by the Learning Standards that are considered sufficient for the expectations \nat this grade.\nNYS Level 2\nStudents performing at this level are partially proficient in standards for their grade. They demonstrate \nknowledge, skills, and practices embodied by the Learning Standards that are considered partial but \ninsufficient for the expectations at this grade. Students performing at Level 2 are considered on track to meet \ncurrent New York high school graduation requirements but are not yet proficient in Learning Standards at \nthis grade.\nNYS Level 1\nStudents performing at this level are below proficient in standards for their grade. They may demonstrate \nlimited knowledge, skills, and practices embodied by the Learning Standards that are considered insufficient\nfor the expectations at this grade. \nPerformance Level Descriptions\nFor information about the Next Generation Mathematics Learning Standards Performance Level \nDescriptions for Grades 3–8, please see the website (https://www.nysed.gov/state-assessment/\nnext-generation-learning-standards-mathematics).\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n6\nDomains, Clusters, Standards, and Sequencing in Instruction and \nAssessment\nThe Grades 3–8 Mathematics Tests will measure the NYS Next Generation Mathematics Learning Standards. \nThe NYS Next Generation Mathematics Learning Standards are divided into standards, clusters, and domains.\n• Standards define what students should understand and be able to do. In some cases, standards are \nfurther articulated into lettered components.\n• Clusters are groups of related standards. Note that standards from different clusters may sometimes \nbe closely related because mathematics is a connected subject.\n• Domains are larger groups of related clusters and standards. Standards from different domains may \nbe closely related.\nSequencing in Instruction and Assessment\nThe New York State Mathematics Grades 3–8 Tests are administered in April/May. Each question on a New \nYork State Mathematics Grades 3–8 grade-level test is aligned only to a pre-test standard (September-to\u0002April/May) for the grade level or a post-test standard (May-to-June) from the prior grade level. While the \npre/post guidance provides a clear designation of when students are assessed for understanding content \nat the proficiency/mastery level, it is not intended to serve as a directive as to when the content should be \nintroduced or how instruction of content should occur.\nThe charts that follow on pages 7–17 of this guide illustrate the relationship between the domains, clusters, \nand standards that comprise each grade level, as well as show the progression of the domains across grade \nlevels. The charts do not indicate any content emphasis. Standards that have been designated as post-test \n(May-to-June) for each grade level are also noted on the respective grade level’s chart. Standards that \nare designated for instruction after the administration of the Grades 3–8 Mathematics Tests will be \nfundamental in ensuring that students are prepared for the instruction of each subsequent grade and \nmay be tested on the subsequent grade level’s test. For more information about the NYS Next Generation \nMathematics Learning Standards Grades 3–8 Post-Test Standards Designations, please refer to the website\n(https://www.nysed.gov/standards-instruction/mathematics-guidance-resources).\nCurriculum and instruction that support the content of the learning standards and the unique learning needs \nof students are locally determined by each individual district in New York State. Teacher preference and \nflexibility in planning units of study continue to play vital roles to both meet the needs of the students and \nalign with the expectations of the learning standards. For additional guidance with instructional planning \nsurrounding the Next Generation Mathematics Learning Standards, please see the Next Generation \nMathematics Learning Standards website (https://www.nysed.gov/standards-instruction/mathematics).\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n7\nGrade 3\nDomain Cluster Standard(s) Post-Test \nStandard\nOperations \nand Algebraic \nThinking\nRepresent and solve problems \ninvolving multiplication and division.\nNY-3.OA.1\nNY-3.OA.2\nNY-3.OA.3\nNY-3.OA.4\nUnderstand properties of \nmultiplication and the relationship \nbetween multiplication and division.\nNY-3.OA.5\nNY-3.OA.6\nMultiply and divide within 100. Students \nwill be provided as much time as necessary within the confines of the regular school day to complete each \ntest session. School personnel should use their best professional judgment and knowledge about individual \nstudents to determine how long a student should be engaged in taking a particular assessment and when it is \nin the student’s best interest to end the test session. \nAlthough test duration will vary among students, the table below provides an average time it will take \nstudents to complete each session. It is strongly encouraged for educators to share the information with \nstudents and parents prior to the test administration. \nAverage Time to\nComplete Session 1\nAverage Time to\nComplete Session 2\nGrade 3 55–65 Minutes 60–70 Minutes\nGrade 4 65–75 Minutes 65–75 Minutes\nGrade 5 80–90 Minutes 70–80 Minutes\nGrade 6 80–90 Minutes 75–85 Minutes\nGrade 7 80–90 Minutes 75–85 Minutes\nGrade 8 80–90 Minutes 75–85 Minutes\nThe tests must be administered under standard conditions and the directions must be followed carefully. The \nsame test administration procedures must be used with all students so that valid inferences can be drawn \nfrom the test results.\nNYSED devotes great attention to the security and integrity of the New York State Testing Program. \nSchool administrators and teachers involved in the administration of State assessments are responsible \nfor understanding and adhering to the instructions set forth in the School Administrator’s Manual and the \nTeacher’s Directions (https://www.nysed.gov/state-assessment/grades-3-8-ela-and-math-test-manuals).\nWhen Students Have Completed Their Tests\nStudents who finish their test should be encouraged to go back and check their work. Once the student has \ncompleted their test, test materials should be collected by the proctor. After a student has submitted the test, \nor their test materials are collected if testing on paper, the student may be permitted to read silently. This \nprivilege is granted at the discretion of each school. No talking and no other schoolwork is permitted.1\nGiven that the spring tests have no time limits, schools and districts have the discretion to create their own \napproach to ensure that all students who are productively working are given the time they need within the \nconfines of the regular school day to continue to take the tests. If the test is administered in a large-group \nsetting, school administrators may prefer to allow students to submit the test, or hand in their test materials if \ntesting on paper, as they finish and then leave the room. If so, take care that students leave the room as quietly \nas possible so as not to disturb the students who are still working on the test.\n1 For more detailed information about test administration, including proper procedures for proctoring, please refer to the \nSchool Administrator’s Manual and the Teacher’s Directions.\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n19\nTest Design\nIn Grades 3–8, students are required to apply mathematical understandings and mathematical practices \ngained in the classroom in order to answer four types of questions: 1-credit multiple-choice questions, \n1-credit constructed-response questions, 2-credit constructed-response questions, and 3-credit constructed\u0002response questions. Session 1 consists of multiple-choice questions. Session 2 consists of multiple-choice \nand constructed-response questions. Students will NOT be permitted to use calculators in Grades 3–5 or \nSession 1 of Grade 6. In Session 2 of Grade 6, students must have the exclusive use of a four-function \ncalculator with a square root key or a scientific calculator. In Grades 7–8, students must have the exclusive \nuse of a scientific calculator for both Session 1 and Session 2. For more information about calculator use, \nplease refer to page 28.\nThe charts below illustrate the test designs for the Grades 3–8 Mathematics Tests. Embedded field test \nquestions are included in the number of multiple-choice and constructed-response questions in Session 1 \nand Session 2 listed below. It will not be apparent to students whether a question is an embedded field test \nquestion that does not count toward their score or an operational test question that does count toward their \nscore.\nGrade 3 Test Design\nSession\nNumber of \nMultiple\u0002Choice \nQuestions\nNumber of \nConstructed\u0002Response \nQuestions \n1-Credit\nNumber of \nConstructed\u0002Response \nQuestions \n2-Credit\nNumber of \nConstructed\u0002Response \nQuestions \n3-Credit\nTotal \nNumber \nof \nQuestions\n1 25 0 0 0 25\n2 5 3 4 1 13\nTotal 30 3 4 1 38\nGrade 4–5 Test Design\nSession\nNumber of \nMultiple\u0002Choice \nQuestions\nNumber of \nConstructed\u0002Response \nQuestions \n1-Credit\nNumber of \nConstructed\u0002Response \nQuestions \n2-Credit\nNumber of \nConstructed\u0002Response \nQuestions \n3-Credit\nTotal \nNumber \nof \nQuestions\n1 30 0 0 0 30\n2 5 3 5 1 14\nTotal 35 3 5 1 44\nGrade 6 Test Design\nSession\nNumber of \nMultiple\u0002Choice \nQuestions\nNumber of \nConstructed\u0002Response \nQuestions \n1-Credit\nNumber of \nConstructed\u0002Response \nQuestions \n2-Credit\nNumber of \nConstructed\u0002Response \nQuestions \n3-Credit\nTotal \nNumber \nof \nQuestions\n1 30 0 0 0 30\n2 6 3 6 1 16\nTotal 36 3 6 1 46\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n20\nGrade 7–8 Test Design\nSession\nNumber of \nMultiple\u0002Choice \nQuestions\nNumber of \nConstructed\u0002Response \nQuestions \n1-Credit\nNumber of \nConstructed\u0002Response \nQuestions \n2-Credit\nNumber of \nConstructed\u0002Response \nQuestions \n3-Credit\nTotal \nNumber \nof \nQuestions\n1 32 0 0 0 32\n2 6 3 6 1 16\nTotal 38 3 6 1 48\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n21\nTest Blueprint\nAll questions on the Grades 3–8 Mathematics Tests measure the Next Generation Mathematics Learning \nStandards. All the content at each grade level is connected to the Standards for Mathematical Practice; \ntherefore, the Grades 3–8 Mathematics Tests will include questions that require students to connect \nmathematical content and mathematical practice. \nWhile all questions are linked to a primary standard, some questions measure more than one standard and \none or more of the Standards for Mathematical Practice. Similarly, some questions measure cluster-level \nunderstandings. As a result of the alignment to standards, clusters, and Standards for Mathematical Practice, \nthe tests assess students’ conceptual understanding, procedural fluency, and problem-solving abilities, rather \nthan assessing their knowledge of isolated skills and facts. \nThe tables below illustrate the domain-level test blueprint percent ranges for each grade. For further detail \nof the scope of grade-level content, please see the grade-level standards charts on pages 7–17 of this guide.\nDomain-Level Test Blueprint—Percent Ranges for Grade 3 Test\nOperations \nand Algebraic \nThinking \nNumber and \nOperations in \nBase Ten\nNumber and \nOperations— \nFractions \nMeasurement \nand Data Geometry\n31–43% 7–14% 18–29% 21–32% 2–8%\nDomain-Level Test Blueprint—Percent Ranges for Grade 4 Test\nOperations \nand Algebraic \nThinking\nNumber and \nOperations in \nBase Ten\nNumber and \nOperations— \nFractions\nMeasurement \nand Data Geometry\n15–25% 20–30% 20–30% 9–14% 13–23%\nDomain-Level Test Blueprint—Percent Ranges for Grade 5 Test\nOperations \nand Algebraic \nThinking\nNumber and \nOperations in \nBase Ten\nNumber and \nOperations— \nFractions\nMeasurement \nand Data Geometry\nPost2 25–35% 34–44% 22–32% 2–7%\nDomain-Level Test Blueprint—Percent Ranges for Grade 6 Test\nRatios and \nProportional \nRelationships\nThe Number \nSystem\nExpressions, \nEquations, and \nInequalities\nGeometry Statistics and \nProbability\n21–30% 17–26% 25–43% 14–24% Post2\n2 All standards in the domain are post-test standards and are not assessed in the current grade level State Assessment. For multiple\u0002choice questions, students select the correct response from four answer choices. For the constructed-response \nquestions, students write an answer to an open-ended question and may be required to show their work. In \nsome cases, they may be required to provide a written explanation for how they arrived at their answers. \nSome test questions target more than one standard or assess an entire cluster. As such, many individual test \nquestions assess September-to-April/May standards in conjunction with May-to-June standards from past \ngrades (i.e., post-test standards).\nMultiple-Choice Questions\nMultiple-choice questions will mainly be used to assess procedural skills and conceptual understanding. \nMany multiple-choice questions require students to complete multiple steps. Likewise, some of these \nquestions are linked to more than one standard, drawing on the simultaneous application of multiple skills \nand concepts. Within answer choices, distractors3 will all be based on plausible missteps. \n1-Credit Constructed-Response Questions\nThe 1-credit constructed-response questions require students to complete a task and provide only their final \nanswer. The 1-credit constructed-response questions will often require multiple steps, assessing procedural \nskills, as well as conceptual understanding and application. While students may show how they arrived to \ntheir final answer, only the final answer will be scored.\n2-Credit Constructed-Response Questions\nThe 2-credit constructed-response questions require students to complete a task and show their work or \nexplain their answer. The 2-credit constructed-response questions will often require multiple steps, the \napplication of multiple mathematics skills, and real-world applications. Many of the 2-credit constructed\u0002response questions will assess conceptual application and understanding.\n3-Credit Constructed-Response Question\nThe 3-credit constructed-response question asks students to show their work or explain their answer in \ncompleting two or more tasks or a more extensive problem. The 3-credit constructed-response question \nallows students to show their understanding of mathematical procedures, conceptual understanding, and \napplication. The 3-credit constructed-response question may also assess student reasoning and the ability to \ncritique the arguments of others. \nAdditional Assessment Resources\nThe New York State Question Sampler (https://www.nysed.gov/state-assessment/question-sampler) \nprovides a preview of the question types in the computer-based testing platform designed to help students \nprepare for testing day using the online testing tools. \n \n3 A distractor is an incorrect response that may appear to be a plausible correct response to a student who has not mastered the skill \nor concept being assessed.\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n24\nMathematics Rubrics and Scoring Policies\nThe Grades 3–8 Mathematics Tests will use the rubrics and scoring policies as shown in this guide. \n1-Credit Constructed-Response Rubric\n1 Credit A 1-credit response is a correct answer to the question which indicates a thorough \nunderstanding of mathematical concepts and/or procedures.\n0 Credits* A 0-credit response is incorrect, irrelevant, or incoherent. \n* Condition Code A is applied whenever a student who is present for a test session leaves an entire \nconstructed-response question in that session completely blank (no response attempted). \n2-Credit Constructed-Response Holistic Rubric\n2 Credits\nA 2-credit response includes the correct solution to the question and demonstrates a \nthorough understanding of the mathematical concepts and/or procedures in the task.\nThis response\n• indicates that the student has completed the task correctly, using mathematically \nsound procedures\n• contains sufficient work to demonstrate a thorough understanding of the \nmathematical concepts and/or procedures\n• may contain inconsequential errors that do not detract from the correct solution \nand the demonstration of a thorough understanding\n1 Credit\nA 1-credit response demonstrates only a partial understanding of the mathematical \nconcepts and/or procedures in the task. \nThis response\n• correctly addresses only some elements of the task \n• may contain an incorrect solution but applies a mathematically appropriate process\n• may contain the correct solution but required work is incomplete\n0 Credits*\nA 0-credit response is incorrect, irrelevant, incoherent, or contains a correct solution \nobtained using an obviously incorrect procedure. Although some elements may contain \ncorrect mathematical procedures, holistically they are not sufficient to demonstrate even \na limited understanding of the mathematical concepts embodied in the task.\n* Condition Code A is applied whenever a student who is present for a test session leaves an entire \nconstructed-response question in that session completely blank (no response attempted). \nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n25\n3-Credit Constructed-Response Holistic Rubric\n3 Credits\nA 3-credit response includes the correct solution(s) to the question and demonstrates a \nthorough understanding of the mathematical concepts and/or procedures in the task.\nThis response\n• indicates that the student has completed the task correctly, using mathematically \nsound procedures\n• contains sufficient work to demonstrate a thorough understanding of the \nmathematical concepts and/or procedures\n• may contain inconsequential errors that do not detract from the correct solution(s) \nand the demonstration of a thorough understanding\n2 Credits\nA 2-credit response demonstrates a partial understanding of the mathematical concepts \nand/or procedures in the task.\nThis response\n• appropriately addresses most but not all aspects of the task using mathematically \nsound procedures \n• may contain an incorrect solution but provides sound procedures, reasoning, and/\nor explanations\n• may reflect some minor misunderstanding of the underlying mathematical concepts \nand/or procedures\n1 Credit\nA 1-credit response demonstrates only a limited understanding of the mathematical \nconcepts and/or procedures in the task.\nThis response\n• may address some elements of the task correctly but reaches an inadequate solution \nand/or provides reasoning that is faulty or incomplete \n• exhibits multiple flaws related to misunderstanding of important aspects of the \ntask, misuse of mathematical procedures, or faulty mathematical reasoning\n• reflects a lack of essential understanding of the underlying mathematical concepts\n• may contain the correct solution(s) but required work is limited\n0 Credits*\nA 0-credit response is incorrect, irrelevant, incoherent, or contains a correct solution \nobtained using an obviously incorrect procedure. Although some elements may contain \ncorrect mathematical procedures, holistically they are not sufficient to demonstrate even \na limited understanding of the mathematical concepts embodied in the task.\n* Condition Code A is applied whenever a student who is present for a test session leaves an entire \nconstructed-response question in that session completely blank (no response attempted).\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n26\nThe following scoring policies must be applied while scoring the mathematics tests for all Grades 3–8. \nThe rubrics for the constructed-response questions are designed to provide a systematic, consistent method \nfor awarding credit. Each response must be rated carefully using the teacher’s professional judgment \nand knowledge of mathematics. Any directions about acceptable formats of answers must be followed \n(e.g., decimal number, rounding, simplest form, in terms of π). If the answer format for a question is not \nspecified, mathematically equivalent solutions should be awarded credit. Please see the scoring materials for \nfurther details on acceptable answer formats specific to individual questions. \n1-Credit Constructed-Response Mathematics Scoring Policies\n1. The student is not required to show work for a 1-credit constructed-response question, therefore, any \nwork shown will not be scored. A clearly identified correct response should still receive full credit. \n2. If the student clearly identifies a correct answer but fails to write that answer in the answer space, the \nstudent should still receive full credit. \n3. If the student provides one legible response (and one response only), the rater should score the \nresponse, even if it has been crossed out. \n4. If the student has written more than one response but has crossed some out, the rater should score \nonly the response that has not been crossed out. \n5. If the student provides more than one response but does not indicate which response is to be considered \nthe correct response and none have been crossed out, the student shall not receive credit. \n6. If the student does not provide the answer in the form as directed in the question, the student will not \nreceive credit. \n7. In questions requiring number sentences, the number sentences must be written horizontally. \n8. When measuring angles with a protractor, there is a +/- 5 degrees deviation allowed of the true \nmeasure. \n9. Condition Code A is applied whenever a student who is present for a test session leaves an entire \nconstructed-response question in that session completely blank (no response attempted). This is not \nto be confused with a score of zero wherein the student does respond to part or all of the question, but \nthat work results in a score of zero.\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n27\n2- and 3-Credit Constructed-Response Mathematics Scoring Policies\n1. If a student shows the work in other than a designated “Show your work” or “Explain” area, that \nwork should still be scored. \n2. If the question requires students to show their work, and the student shows appropriate work and \nclearly identifies a correct answer but fails to write that answer in the answer space, the student \nshould still receive full credit.\n3. If students are directed to show work or provide an explanation, a correct answer with no work \nshown or no explanation provided, receives no credit.\n4. If students are not directed to show work, any work shown will not be scored. This applies to \nquestions that do not ask for any work and questions that ask for work for one part and do not ask \nfor work in another part.\n5. If the student provides one legible response (and one response only), the rater should score the \nresponse, even if it has been crossed out.\n6. If the student has written more than one response but has crossed some out, the rater should score \nonly the response that has not been crossed out.\n7. If the student provides more than one response, but does not indicate which response is to be considered \nthe correct response and none have been crossed out, the student shall not receive full credit.\n8. Trial-and-error responses are not subject to Scoring Policy #6 above, since crossing out is part of the \ntrial-and-error process.\n9. If a response shows repeated occurrences of the same conceptual error within a question, the conceptual \nerror should not be considered more than once in gauging the demonstrated level of understanding.\n10. In questions requiring number sentences, the number sentences must be written horizontally.\n11. When measuring angles with a protractor, there is a +/- 5 degrees deviation allowed of the true \nmeasure.\n12. Condition Code A is applied whenever a student who is present for a test session leaves an entire \nconstructed-response question in that session completely blank (no response attempted). This is not \nto be confused with a score of zero wherein the student does respond to part or all of the question but \nthat work results in a score of zero.\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n28\nMathematics Tools\nWhy Mathematics Tools?\nThese provisions are necessary for students to meet Standard for Mathematical Practice Five found throughout \nthe New York State Next Generation Mathematics Learning Standards:\nUse appropriate tools strategically\nMathematically proficient students consider the available tools when solving a mathematical \nproblem. Proficient students are sufficiently familiar with tools appropriate for their grade or \ncourse to make sound decisions about when each of these tools might be helpful, recognizing \nboth the insight to be gained and their limitations. Mathematically proficient students at various \ngrade levels are able to identify relevant external mathematical resources, such as digital content \nlocated on a website, and use them to pose or solve problems. They are able to use technological \ntools to explore and deepen their understanding of concepts.\nIt is up to the student to decide when it will be helpful to use the mathematics tools to answer a question.\nRulers and Protractors \nStudents in Grade 3 must have a ruler for their exclusive use for both sessions of the test. Students in \nGrades 4–8 must have a ruler and a protractor for their exclusive use for both sessions of the test. Students \nwith disabilities may use adapted rulers and protractors if this is indicated as a testing accommodation on the \nstudent’s Individualized Education Program or Section 504 Accommodation Plan.\nNote: Schools are responsible for supplying the appropriate tools for use with the Grades 3–8 Mathematics \nTests when testing with printed test booklets. A ruler tool and a protractor tool are provided to students as \npart of the computer-based testing delivery system, Nextera.\nCalculators\nStudents in Grades 3–5 are NOT permitted to use a calculator on the Mathematics Tests.\nStudents in Grade 6 are NOT permitted to use a calculator with Session 1. For Session 2, students should have \nexclusive use of a four-function calculator with a square root key or a scientific calculator. Graphing \ncalculators are NOT permitted.\nStudents in Grades 7–8 should have exclusive use of a scientific calculator for both Session 1 and \nSession 2. Graphing calculators are NOT permitted.\nFor students testing on computers in Grades 6–8, a calculator is provided as part of the computer-based \ntesting delivery system, but schools should continue to supply students with exclusive use of the type of \nhandheld calculator the students use for everyday mathematics instruction.\nValue of Pi\nStudents should learn that π is an irrational number. For the constructed-response questions in Grades 7–8 \n(Session 2), the π key and the full display of the calculator should be used in computations. The approximate \nvalues of π, such as 3.1416, 3.14, or 22\n7\n, are unacceptable. \nMore information on mathematics tool specifications can be found in the School Administrator’s \nManual, Appendix E, located on the OSA website (https://www.nysed.gov/state-assessment/grades-3-8-\nela-and-math-test-manuals).\nGrades 3–8 Mathematics Educator Guide\n29\nReference Sheets\nEach student testing in Grades 5–8 will be provided with a mathematics reference sheet for their exclusive \nuse during both Session 1 and Session 2. It is recommended that throughout the year, teachers provide \nstudents opportunities during classroom instruction to gain familiarity with the grade-level reference sheet. \nNote: Due to certain standards’ expectations for Grades 7–8, conversion factors that go across measurement \nsystems are also provided. Students should utilize these conversion factors provided on the reference sheet \neven though some of the conversion factors shown may not be exact.\nGrade 5 Mathematics Reference Sheet
