Tables M1–M12 in Appendix M show the omit \nrates for items on the Grades 3–8 ELA and Mathematics Tests. The industry standard general \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n41 \n \n \n \n \nrule is that omit rates for multiple-choice items should be less than 5%; omit rates for items on \nthe Grades 3–8 ELA and Mathematics Tests were less than 1%. \nDifferential Item Functioning (DIF) \nClassical differential item functioning (DIF) analyses are statistical methods for identifying items \nthat are estimated to have functioned differently for one group (i.e., the “focal” group) as \ncompared with another group (i.e., the “reference” group). In other words, DIF analysis only \nflags items that may later be judged by content experts to exhibit bias rather than directly \ndetecting bias. The psychometric phenomenon of DIF has been extensively investigated, and \nexperts’ judgments of bias were collected when items were field tested, which reduced the \nlikelihood of including any differentially functioning items on the operational forms. DIF was \nevaluated for operational items using two methods: the Mantel-Haenszel Delta method (Dorans, \n& Holland, 1992) for dichotomous items and the standardized mean difference method (Dorans \net al., 1992) for polytomous items. Please refer to the NYSTP 2024: ELA and Mathematics \nGrades 3–8 Field Test Technical Report for details about these DIF methods and item-flagging \ncriteria. Operational items flagged for DIF are given additional scrutiny by content specialists \n(above and beyond the existing rounds of reviews by New York State educators) to identify \npotential systematic issues that could be addressed in future item writing. \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n42 \n \n \n \n \nSection 6: IRT Calibration \n6.1. IRT Models and Rationale for Use \nItem response theory (IRT) allows for comparisons between items and scale scores, even those \nfrom different test forms, by using a common scale for all items and examinees (i.e., as if there \nwere a hypothetical test that contained items from all forms). \nIRT is a set of statistical models that attempt to relate observed responses to items on a test to \nlatent traits. In the case of educational tests, the latent trait of interest is often students’ mastery \nof a particular discipline, such as mathematics. Computer programs that implement IRT models \nuse student data to estimate the characteristics of the items on a test, called “parameters.” The \nparameter-estimation process is called “item calibration.” \nIRT models typically vary according to the number of parameters estimated. For the New York \nState tests, two types of item parameters are estimated: the discrimination parameter and the \ndifficulty parameter(s). The discrimination parameter is an index of how well an item \ndifferentiates between high-performing and low-performing students. An item that cannot be \nanswered correctly by low-performing students but can be answered correctly by high\u0002performing students will have a high discrimination value. The difficulty parameter is an index \nof how easy or difficult an item is. The higher the difficulty parameter is, the harder the item is. \nDichotomous and polytomous items differ in their number of score categories. To accommodate \nthese differences, separate IRT models are used in item calibration. The two-parameter logistic \n(2PL) model (Lord, 1980; Lord & Novick, 1968) was adopted in 2023 for analyzing \ndichotomous items. In this model, the probability that a student with proficiency θ responds \ncorrectly to item i is: \n1\n𝑃𝑃𝑖𝑖(𝜃𝜃) = 1 + 𝑒𝑒𝑒𝑒𝑒𝑒 (−𝐷𝐷𝑎𝑎𝑖𝑖 𝑖𝑖 (𝜃𝜃 − 𝑏𝑏 )) \nwhere D is a scaling constant of 1.7, and ai and bi are the discrimination and difficulty \nparameters of item i, respectively. \nFor analysis of the polytomous items, the generalized partial-credit (GPC) model (Muraki, 1992) \nis used. In this model, the probability of an examinee with proficiency 𝜃𝜃 obtaining score k on \nitem i is: \n𝑖𝑖 𝑒𝑒𝑒𝑒𝑒𝑒 (∑ 𝐷𝐷𝑎𝑎𝑖𝑖(𝜃𝜃 − 𝑏𝑏𝑖𝑖 + 𝑑𝑑𝑖𝑖ℎ) ℎ=0 𝑃𝑃𝑖𝑖𝑖𝑖(𝜃𝜃) = 𝑃𝑃(𝑒𝑒𝑖𝑖 = 𝑘𝑘) = \n∑𝑚𝑚𝑖𝑖 𝑐𝑐 𝑒𝑒𝑒𝑒𝑒𝑒 (∑ 𝐷𝐷𝑎𝑎𝑖𝑖 (𝜃𝜃 − 𝑏𝑏𝑖𝑖 + 𝑑𝑑𝑖𝑖ℎ) 𝑐𝑐=0 ℎ=0\nwhere: \n• D, ai, and bi have been previously defined, \n• mi is the maximum number of score points of item i, and \n• dih is the category parameter of item i on score h. \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n43 \n \n \n \nThere are mi -1 independent category parameters for item i because di0 is usually arbitrarily set to \n𝑖𝑖 0, and the remaining category parameters satisfy ∑𝑖𝑖=1\n𝑚𝑚 𝑑𝑑𝑖𝑖𝑖𝑖 = 0. \n6.2. Calibration Sample \nThe cleaned data were used to calibrate the NYSTP 2024 Grades 3–8 ELA and Mathematics \nTests. Calibration sample sizes were adequate, as the calibration was performed using nearly all \nthe New York State public and non-public school student population data in each grade. As \nshown in Tables 6.1–6.3 and Tables 6.4–6.6 for ELA and mathematics, respectively, the 2024 \noperational test samples were generally comparable to the 2023 populations in terms of student \ngender, race and ethnicity, NRC category, proportions of ELLs, proportions of students with \ndisabilities, and proportions of students using testing accommodations. \nTable 6.1. Dichotomous and polytomous items were calibrated \nsimultaneously using marginal maximum likelihood procedures. \nThe calibration of NYSTP 2024 Grades 3–8 ELA and Mathematics Tests did not exhibit any \ntest-level issues. The estimated parameters were on the original theta scale, and all items were \nwell within the prescribed parameter ranges except for a few, such as one Grade 4 mathematics \nitem (a = 0.11). All calibration estimation results were reasonable for the Grades 3–8 ELA and \nMathematics Tests. Table 6.7 and Table 6.8 present the summaries of the calibration results for \nELA and mathematics, respectively. Additional details, including individual item parameter \nestimates, can be found in Appendix N in Tables N1–N12. The parameter estimates are \nexpressed on the theta metric and are defined as: \n• Dichotomous items: \no a is a discrimination parameter. \no b is a difficulty parameter. \n• Polytomous items: \no a is an item discrimination parameter. \no b is an item location parameter. \no dk is the item category parameter for category k. \nTable 6.7. Item-Model Fit \nThe Standards for Educational and Psychological Testing suggests documenting evidence of \nmodel fit when model-based methods such as IRT are used to estimate item parameters in test \ndevelopment. The standard process of assessing the fit of an item under unidimensional IRT \nmodels involves steps such as (a) defining a number of examinee groups (“buckets”) and then (b) \nmaking an informed judgment by comparing the observed and model-predicted proportion\u0002correct scores for the item by the examinees in different “buckets” (Sinharay, 2006). To make \nthis judgment on each item, Hambleton and Swaminathan (1985) recommend the use of \ngraphical plots comparing the estimated/predicted item-response function to the empirical \nstudent-response data for an item. An example item fit plot is shown in Figure 6.1. \nFigure 6.1. Example Item Fit Plot \nFit plots were produced and closely examined for all operational items to visually examine the \nmodel-data fit for each item. All items showed adequate model-data fit except for one item in \nGrade 4 mathematics. This fact further supports the use of the chosen IRT models. \n6.4. Equating \nAn IRT-based equating procedure was conducted for all ELA and mathematics tests to place the \n2024 calibrated item parameter estimates on the base scale established in 2023. Multiple-choice \nitems on a 2024 operational form that were field tested in an embedded setting in previous \nadministrations were used as anchor items for equating. Anchor items consisted of 48–63% of \nthe total score points and 59–75% of the operational items across subjects and grade levels. \nThese substantial proportions of anchor items help stabilize equating results because a higher \nnumber of common items tends to result in a less random equating error (Budescu, 1985; \nWingersky et al., 1987). \nOnce the item calibrations were completed, the anchor items were used to equate the form onto \nthe base scale using a common-item nonequivalent groups design. The banked IRT parameters of \nthe anchor items were used as reference parameters in the 2024 post-equating process. \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n50 \n \n \n \n \n \n \nThe Stocking-Lord (1983) procedure, as implemented by the STUIRT computer program (Kim \n& Kolen, 2004), was used to obtain the equating coefficients needed for the linear transformation \nof each new form onto the base scale. \nThe stability of the common items used in the Stocking-Lord procedure was assessed using the \nweighted, squared deviations between the item characteristic curves (ICCs) for the new and \nreference administrations. For a given item, i, the deviation called “d-squared” was calculated \nusing the following equation: \ndi \n2 = ∑ {[Pri,new (θk ) − Pri,ref (θk )]2\n.g(θ k )} k \nwhere 𝑖𝑖 indexes anchor items, 𝑘𝑘 indexes quadrature points for 𝜃𝜃, Pr𝑖𝑖,ref(∙) is the probability of a \ncorrect response to item 𝑖𝑖 under the previous administrations while Pr𝑖𝑖 ,new(∙) is the same \nquantity based on the current administration’s on-scale item parameters, and 𝑔𝑔(𝜃𝜃𝑖𝑖) are weights \nfor each of the 𝐾𝐾 quadrature points. \nAn anchor item with the largest 𝑑𝑑𝑖𝑖\n2 > 0.01 would be flagged and considered for removal from an \nanchor set during an iterative equating process. Besides the d² criterion, the following item \nstatistics were evaluated for anchor item stability: classical item statistics (p value and point\u0002biserial correlations), item difficulty analysis, item fit evaluation, and ICC plot. After removing \nan anchor item, an impact analysis was also conducted based on equating results from the \nremaining anchor set. Following the stability checks and impact analysis, NWEA retained all \nanchor items for ELA tests and removed one anchor item from each mathematics test, with \nNYSED’s approval. \nTable 6.9 and Table 6.10 present the resulting equating coefficients. The following parameters \nwere equated using the formulas below: \nC\nE ai ai = M E \n1 \n𝐸𝐸 𝐸𝐸 𝑏𝑏𝑖𝑖 = (𝑀𝑀1\n𝐸𝐸𝑏𝑏𝑖𝑖\n𝐶𝐶) + 𝑀𝑀2\nE C E d ij ij = d M1 \nwhere M1 \nE is defined as the multiplicative adjustment for linking, and M2 E is the additive \nadjustment for linking. The superscript “E” denotes linked item parameter estimates, while the \nsuperscript “C” denotes calibrated item parameter estimates. \nTable 6.9. Scaling and Scoring Procedure \nNew York State student examinations were scored using the number correct (NC) scoring \nmethod. This method considers how many score points a student obtained on a test in \ndetermining their scale score. That is, two students with the same score points on the test will \nreceive the same scale score, regardless of which items they answered correctly. In this method, \nthe number correct (or raw) score on the test is converted to a scale score by means of a \nconversion table. This traditional scoring method is often preferred for its conceptual simplicity \nand familiarity. \nThe final item parameters were used to calculate the raw-score-to-theta-score tables using a test\n𝑆𝑆 characteristic curve (TCC) method. The obtained scaling transformation slope and intercept, 𝑀𝑀1\nand 𝑀𝑀2\n𝑆𝑆, from the 2023 standard setting were then applied to the theta values to produce raw\u0002score-to-scale-score conversion tables for the Grades 3–8 ELA and Mathematics Tests. \nThe inverse TCC method was employed using POLYEQUATE (Kolen, 2004). The inverse of \nthe TCC procedure produces trait values (i.e., proficiency) based on unweighted raw scores. \nThese estimates show negligible statistical bias (defined in statistics as the difference between an \nestimator’s expected value and the true value of the parameter being estimated) for tests with \nmaximum possible raw scores of at least 30 points. All NYSTP Grade 3–8 ELA and \nMathematics Tests have a maximum raw score higher than 30 points. In the inverse TCC \nmethod, a student’s trait (i.e., proficiency) estimate is taken to be the trait value that has an \nexpected raw score equal to the student’s observed raw score. It was found that for tests \ncontaining only MC items, the inverse TCC is an excellent first-order approximation of the \nnumber of correct maximum likelihood estimates (MLEs). For tests with a mixture of MC and \nCR items, the MLE and TCC estimates are even more similar (Yen, 1984). \nThe inverse TCC method relies on the following equation: \nn n \nΣ vii x = Σ vE i (X i θ\n) i=1 i=1 \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n52 \n \n \n \nwhere: \n• xi is a student’s observed raw score on item i, \n• vi is a non-optimal weight specified in a scoring process ( vi = 1 if no weights are \nspecified), and \n• is a trait estimate. \nWith the adoption of the 2PL model, the θ scores can be obtained for all raw score points, except \nfor zero and perfect scores, using the inverse TCC method. \nAfter the raw-score-to-theta-score conversion tables were produced, adjustments were made to \nthe θ scores at the two ends of the scale based on the smoothing rules specified in Table 6.11. \nThese adjustments were to smooth the unreliable θ scores with relatively large conditional \nstandard errors of measurement (CSEMs). \nTable 6.11. Smoothing Rules \nSubject Grade \nSmoothing \nStarting Point Step Size \nELA \nMath \n3–8 \n3–8 \nCSEM > 0.56 0.16 \nCSEM > 0.44 0.12 \nAt both ends of the scale, for any theta estimates with CSEMs greater than 0.56 for ELA and \n0.44 for mathematics, 0.16 and 0.12 were subtracted (at the low end) or added (at the high end) \nfrom the preceding theta value for ELA and mathematics, respectively. Table 6.12 shows an \nexample of smoothing at the two ends of the ELA and mathematics tests. \nTable 6.12. Mathematics Grade 8 CSEM Curve \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n61 \nSection 7: Reliability and Standard Error of Measurement \nThis section presents specific information on various test reliability statistics, standard errors of \nmeasurement (SEMs), and the results of performance level classification accuracy and \nconsistency analyses. The data set for these analyses includes New York State students who were \ntested and received valid scores. \n7.1. Test Reliability \nTest reliability is directly related to score stability and standard error and, as such, is an essential \nelement of fairness and validity. Test reliability can be directly measured with an alpha statistic, \nand the alpha statistic can be used to derive the SEM. For the Grades 3–8 ELA and Mathematics \nTests, NWEA calculated two types of reliability statistics: Cronbach’s alpha (Cronbach, 1951) \nand the Feldt-Raju coefficient (Qualls, 1995). These two measures are appropriate for assessing \nthe internal consistency of a test when a single test is administered to a group of examinees on \none occasion. The reliability of the test is then estimated by considering how well the items \nreflecting the same construct yield similar results (or how consistent the results are for different \nitems that reflect the same construct measured by the test). Both Cronbach’s alpha and the Feldt\u0002Raju coefficient measures are appropriate for tests consisting of multiple item formats (MC and \nCR items). \nTest Statistics and Reliability for Total Test \nTable 7.1 and Table 7.3 present the test statistics, including raw-score (RS) means and raw-score \nstandard deviations (SDs) for ELA and mathematics, respectively. Table 7.2 and Table 7.4 \npresent the case counts (“N-Count”), number of test items (“#Items”), Cronbach’s alpha and \nassociated SEM, and the Feldt-Raju coefficient and associated SEM obtained for the total ELA \nand mathematics tests. Reliability coefficients provide measures of internal consistency that \nrange from zero to one. High reliability indicates that scores are consistent and not unduly \ninfluenced by random error. The total test reliability is a very good indication of each test’s \ninternal consistency. \nGrades 3–8 ELA reliability estimates (Cronbach’s alpha and Feldt-Raju coefficient) ranged from \n0.87 to 0.92. Grades 3–8 mathematics reliability estimates (Cronbach’s alpha and Feldt-Raju \ncoefficient) ranged from 0.91 to 0.94. The reliabilities were similar across grades and slightly \nhigher for the mathematics tests than for the ELA tests. All reliabilities were at least 0.87 across \nall grades and both subjects, which is a good indication that the NYSTP Grades 3–8 ELA and \nMathematics Tests are acceptably reliable. \nTable 7.1. Results should be interpreted with caution because the number of items is small. \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n64 \n \n \n \n \nTest Reliability for Subgroups \nIn this section, reliability coefficients that were estimated for the population and subgroups are \npresented. The subgroups include the following: gender, ethnicity, Needs Resource Capacity \n(NRC) category, English Language Learner (ELL) status, all students with disabilities (SWDs), \nall students using test accommodations (SUAs), SWD/SUA (includes examinees who are \nclassified as having a disability and who use at least one disability-related accommodation), and \nELLs using accommodations specific to their ELL status (ELL/SUA). Accommodations \navailable to students include Flexibility in Scheduling/Timing, Flexibility in Setting, Method of \nPresentation (excluding braille), Method of Response, Braille and Large-type, and others (IEP or \n504 Plan). Accommodations available to ELLs are Separate Location and Bilingual Dictionary. \nAs shown in Tables 7.9–7.14 and Tables 7.15–7.20 for ELA and mathematics, respectively, the \nestimated reliabilities for subgroups were close in magnitude to the test reliability estimates of \nthe population. Except for the non-binary group, Cronbach’s alpha reliability coefficients were \nall at least 0.74, and the Feldt-Raju reliability coefficients, which tend to be larger than \nCronbach’s alpha estimates for the same group, were at least 0.76. These indicate a very good \ninternal test consistency (reliability) for the analyzed subgroups of examinees. \nTable 7.9. Standard Error of Measurement (SEM) \nTable 7.2 and Table 7.4 presented the SEMs computed from Cronbach’s alpha and the Feldt\u0002Raju reliability statistics for ELA and mathematics, respectively. The SEMs ranged from 2.43 to \n3.17 across subjects, grades, and the two estimation methods, which were reasonable and small. \nThe SEMs are directly related to reliability: the higher the reliability, the lower the standard \nerror. As discussed, the reliability of these tests is relatively high, so the SEMs were expected to \nbe low. \nThe SEMs for the subpopulations, as computed from Cronbach’s alpha and the Feldt-Raju \nreliability statistics, were presented in Tables 7.9–7.14 and Tables 7.15–7.20 for ELA and \nmathematics, respectively. The SEMs associated with all reliability estimates for all subjects, \ngrades, estimation methods, and subpopulations, except for the non-binary group, ranged from \n2.33 to 3.23, which were close to those for the entire population. This narrow range indicates that \nall students’ test scores are reasonably reliable across the Grades 3–8 ELA and Mathematics \nTests with minimal error. \n7.3. Performance Level Classification Consistency and Accuracy \nClassification consistency refers to the estimated degree of agreement between examinees’ \nperformance classification from two independent administrations of the same test (or from two \nparallel forms of the test). Because obtaining test scores from two independent administrations of \nNew York State tests was not feasible due to item release after each administration, a \npsychometric model was used to obtain the estimated classification consistency indices using test \nscores from a single administration. Classification accuracy can be defined as the agreement \nbetween the actual classifications using observed cut scores and true classifications based on \nknown true cut scores (Livingston & Lewis, 1995). \nIn conjunction with measures of internal consistency, classification consistency is an important \ntype of reliability and is particularly relevant to high-stakes tests. As a form of reliability, \nclassification consistency represents the extent to which a student’s performance classification is \nexpected to remain the same over repeated measurements. \nClassification consistency is most relevant for students whose performance is near the \nproficiency cut score. For example, consider the cut score delineating Levels 2 and 3, or simply \nthe “Level 3 cut.” Students whose proficiency is far above or far below that cut score are \nunlikely to be misclassified because repeated administration of the test will nearly always result \nin the same classification. Students whose true scores are close to the cut score are a more \nserious concern. These students’ true scores will likely lie within the SEM of the cut score. For \nthis reason, the measurement error at the cut scores should be considered when evaluating the \nclassification consistency of a test. Furthermore, the number of students near the cut scores \nshould also be considered when evaluating classification consistency, as these numbers show the \nnumber of students who are at risk of being misclassified. \nScoring tables with SEMs and student scale score frequency distributions are located in \nAppendix P. Classification consistency and accuracy were estimated using the IRT procedure \nsuggested by Lee et al. (2002) and Wang et al. (2000). Appendix O includes a description of the \ncalculations and procedure based on the paper by Lee et al. (2002). \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n74 \n \n \n \nConsistency \nThe results for classifying students into four performance levels are separated from those based \nsolely on the Level 3 cut. Table 7.21 and Table 7.22 include case counts (“N-Count”), \nclassification consistency (“Agreement”), classification inconsistency (“Inconsistency”), and \nCohen’s kappa (“Kappa”). Consistency indicates the rate at which a second administration would \nyield the same performance category designation (or a different designation for the inconsistency \nrate). The agreement index is a sum of the diagonal elements in the contingency table. Kappa is a \nsimilar measure but corrects for chance agreement. The inconsistency index is equal to the \n“1 - agreement index.” \nTable 7.21 depicts the ELA and mathematics consistency study results based on the range of \nperformance levels for all grades. For ELA, 64–68% of students were estimated to be classified \nconsistently into one of the four performance categories following a hypothetical second \nadministration. Kappa coefficients—which correct for chance agreement—ranged from 0.51 to \n0.56. These values are between “moderate” and “substantial” agreement per Landis and Koch’s \n(1977) rules of thumb for kappa. For mathematics, 72–75% of students were estimated to be \nclassified consistently into one of the four performance categories, and kappa coefficients ranged \nfrom 0.62 to 0.64. These values are all considered “substantial” agreement by Landis and Koch’s \n(1977) rules of thumb for the kappa statistic. \nAs mentioned above, all scores contain an acceptable measurement error for all tests. For \nexample, by random chance, students testing twice may be classified first as Level 3 and second \nas Level 4. This is expected to occur more often for students scoring around a specific cut score \nand less often for students scoring closer to the middle of a performance level (i.e., close to the \nmid-point of two adjacent cut scores). \nTable 7.21. By definition, \naccuracy associated with the Level 3 cut is at least as great as that with the entire set of cut scores \nbecause there are only two categories for the former, as opposed to the latter, which has four. \nFor ELA, the estimated accuracy rates indicate that the categorization of a student’s observed \nperformance agrees with the location of their underlying proficiency 72% to 75% of the time \nacross all performance levels and 89% to 91% of the time regarding the Level 3 cut score. These include the scale-score means, standard deviations, and \nperformance level distributions for each grade’s population and subgroups. Demographic \nsubgroups include gender, ethnicity, Needs Resource Capacity (NRC) category, English \nLanguage Learner (ELL) status, students with disabilities (SWDs), and students using test \naccommodations (SUAs). Furthermore, the ELL/SUA subgroup is defined as ELLs who use one \nor more ELL-related accommodation, and the SWD/SUA subgroup is defined as SWDs who use \none or more disability-related accommodation. For the mathematics analyses, the test translation \nlanguage is also indicated (ELA tests are not translated, as they are a measure of mastery of the \nEnglish language.) ELA and mathematics data include examinees with valid scores from all \npublic, non-public, and charter schools. Complete scale-score frequency distribution tables for \nELA and mathematics are located in Appendix P. \n8.1. Scale Score Distribution Summary \nThe following two subsections present ELA and mathematics scale scores and subscore statistics \nby grade and selected subgroups. Some \ngeneral observations from these tables include: \n• Female students outperformed Male students. \n• Asian students scored considerably higher than other reported ethnic groups. \n• Students from Low Needs (as identified by NRC category) districts outperformed \nstudents from other districts (New York City, Big 4 Cities, Urban/Suburban, Rural, \nAverage Needs, and Charter). \n• ELLs, SWDs, and/or SUAs tended to underperform the State population (All Students). \nTable 8.1. ELA Grade 7 \nTable 8.7 presents the Grade 7 scale-score statistics and n-counts for key demographic \nsubgroups. The population scale-score mean was 447.62, with a standard deviation of 22.82. \nFemale students tended to outperform Male students by 6 scale-score points. Asian, Multiracial, \nPacific Islander, and White students’ scale-score means exceeded the State mean scale score, as \ndid those of students from New York City, Low Needs districts, and Charter schools. Across \nethnic groups, Asian students earned the highest mean score (14 scale-score points above the \nState population), and Hispanic students earned the lowest mean score (5 scale-score points \nbelow the State population). Across NRC subgroups, students from Low Needs districts earned \nthe highest mean scale score (8 scale-score points above the State population), and students from \nBig 4 Cities districts earned the lowest mean score (12 scale-score points below the State \npopulation). The SWD, SUA, and ELL subgroups scored about 15–29 scale-score points below \nthe mean scale score for the population. ELLs were the lowest-performing subgroup analyzed, \nscoring about 29 scale-score points below the State mean. \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n83 \n \n \n \n \nTable 8.7. Tables 8.11–8.16 summarize the scale scores by subgroup for mathematics. Some \ngeneral observations from the mathematics data include: \n• Male students outperformed Female students in Grades 3–5 but underperformed Female \nstudents in Grade 8, while performance among the groups was roughly equal in Grades 6 \nand 7. \n• Asian students scored considerably higher than other reported ethnic groups. \n• Students from Low Needs districts (as identified by NRC category) outperformed \nstudents from High Needs (New York City, Big 4 Cities, Urban/Suburban, and Rural) \nand Average Needs districts. \n• ELLs, SWDs, and/or SUAs tended to underperform the State population (All Students). \n• Students taking the Chinese (Simplified) translation tended to outperform those taking \nother translations. \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n85 \n \nThe n-counts for some translation subgroups were low, and the results might have been heavily \ninfluenced by very high- and/or very low-achieving individual students. Mathematics Grade 3 \nTable 8.11 presents the Grade 3 scale-score statistics and n-counts for key demographic \nsubgroups. The population scale-score mean was 451.21, with a standard deviation of 26.34. \nMale students outperformed Female students by 2 scale-score points. Asian, Multiracial, Pacific \nIslander, and White students’ scale-score means exceeded the State mean scale score, as did \nthose of students enrolled in New York City, Low Needs districts, and Charter schools. Across \nethnic groups, Asian students earned the highest mean score (15 scale-score points above the \nState population), and Hispanic students earned the lowest mean score (6 scale-score points \nbelow the State population). Across NRC subgroups, students from Low Needs districts earned \nthe highest mean scale score (11 scale-score points above the State population), and students \nfrom Big 4 Cities districts earned the lowest mean score (16 scale-score points below the State \npopulation). The SWD, SUA, and ELL subgroups scored 16–21 scale-score points below the \nmean scale score for the population. ELLs tested under accommodations were the lowest\u0002performing subgroup analyzed for English forms, scoring about 25 scale-score points below the \nState mean. Among ELLs taking the language translation forms, the mean scale score was the \nhighest for the Chinese (Simplified) subgroup and the lowest for the Haitian-Creole subgroup. \nTable 8.11. Mathematics Grade 8 \nTable 8.16 presents the Grade 8 scale-score statistics and n-counts for key demographic \nsubgroups. The population scale-score mean was 447.18, with a standard deviation of 27.79. \nFemale students tended to outperform Male students by 2 scale-score points. Asian, Pacific \nIslander, and White students’ scale-score means exceeded the State mean scale score, as did \nthose of students enrolled in New York City, Low Needs districts, Charter, and Religious or \nIndependent schools. Across ethnic groups, Asian students earned the highest mean score (20 \nscale-score points above the State population), and African-American students earned the lowest \nmean score (7 scale-score points below the State population). Across NRC subgroups, students \nfrom Religious or Independent schools earned the highest mean scale score (12 scale-score \npoints above the State population), and students from Big 4 Cities districts earned the lowest \nmean score (17 scale-score points below the State population). The SWD, SUA, and ELL \nsubgroups scored 16–18 scale-score points below the mean scale score for the population. ELLs \ntested under accommodations were the lowest-performing subgroup analyzed for English forms, \nscoring about 26 scale-score points below the State mean. Among ELLs taking the language \ntranslation forms, the mean scale score was the highest for the Chinese (Simplified) subgroup \nand the lowest for the Haitian-Creole subgroup. \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n93 \n \n \nTable 8.16. Performance Level Distribution Summary \nStudents under the New York State Testing Program are classified into performance levels as \nLevel 1, Level 2, Level 3, or Level 4. The cut scores for these performance levels were \nestablished during the standards review in the summer of 2023. While vertical articulation helps \napply consistent meaning to the performance levels, the very nature of grade-specific content, \ndiffering performance expectations, and panel-set cut scores result in cut-score differences across \ngrades. Students are considered proficient if they are classified as Level 3 or Level 4. \nELA Test Performance Level Distributions \nTable 8.17 shows the performance level distributions for all examinees for each ELA grade. \nTables 8.18 through 8.23 show the performance level distributions by subgroup for each ELA \ngrade. The percentage of proficient students at a subgroup level reflected the mean scale-score \ndistributions for the subgroup. Therefore, similar achievement trends were observed for \nthe percentage of proficient students: \n• Female students outperformed Male students. \n• Asian students outperformed other ethnic groups. \n• Students from Low Needs (as identified by NRC category) districts outperformed \nstudents from other districts (New York City, Big 4 Cities, Urban/Suburban, Rural, \nAverage Needs, and Charter). \n• ELLs, SWDs, and/or SUAs tended to underperform the State population (All Students). \nTable 8.17. Tables 8.25 through 8.30 show the performance level distributions by subgroup for each \nmathematics grade. The percentage of proficient students at a subgroup level reflected the mean \nscale-score distributions for the subgroup. Therefore, similar achievement trends were observed \nfor the percentage of proficient students: \n• Gender performance was mixed. Male students outperformed Female students in Grades \n3–5 but underperformed Female students in Grade 8, while performance among the \ngroups was roughly equal in Grades 6 and 7. \n• Asian students outperformed other ethnic groups. \n• Students from Low Needs (as identified by NRC category) districts outperformed \nstudents from other districts (New York City, Big 4 Cities, Urban/Suburban, Rural, \nAverage Needs, and Charter). \n• ELLs, SWDs, and/or SUAs tended to underperform the State population (All Students). \n• The subgroup that used the Chinese (Simplified) translations outperformed other test \ntranslation subgroups. \nThe n-counts for some translation subgroups were low, and the results might have been heavily \ninfluenced by very high- and/or very low-achieving individual students. Therefore, the summary \nof proficient percentages for the translation subgroups was limited to subgroups with a minimum \nn-count of 100. \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n102 \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \nTable 8.24. \n8.2.2.6. Mathematics Grade 8 \nTable 8.30 presents the mathematics Grade 8 performance level summaries and n-counts for key \ndemographic subgroups. The percentage of proficient students was 43.50% for the State \npopulation. That percentage was 4% higher for Female students than for Male students. \nCompared with the State population, the percentages of proficient students were higher for \nAsian, Multiracial, Pacific Islander, and White students; the same is true for students enrolled in \nNew York City, Low Needs districts, Charter schools, or Religious or Independent schools. \nAcross ethnic groups, the percentage of proficient students was the highest for Asian students \n(25% above the State population) and the lowest for African-American students (10% below the \nState population). Across NRC subgroups, the percentage of proficient students was the highest \nfor Low Needs districts (19% above the State population) and the lowest for Big 4 Cities districts \n(about 25% below the State population). The percentages of proficient students for SWD, SUA, \nand ELL subgroups were about 25–28% below that for the State population. ELLs tested under \naccommodations had the lowest percentage of proficient students, 37% below that for the State \npopulation. Among ELLs taking the language translation forms, the percentage of proficient \nstudents was the lowest for the Spanish subgroup and the highest for the Chinese (Simplified) \nsubgroup. \nTable 8.30. The foundation for preparing \nstudents for the linguistic rigors of college and of the workplace lies in the texts with which they \ninteract. By the time they graduate, students should be prepared to successfully read and analyze \nthe types of complex texts they will encounter after high school. Selecting passages of \nappropriate type and complexity for use in assessment is integral to this preparation. \nThe New York State Next Generation Learning Standards for ELA emphasize developing skills \nfor comprehending and analyzing both literary and informational texts. Increased exposure to \ninformational texts better prepares students for the various types of texts they will encounter in \ncollege and in the workplace. The array of passages selected for assessment in K–12 should \nsupport the development of the necessary skills to handle a range of literary and informational \ntexts. \nIn addition to the usual fairness and sensitivity guidelines for selecting passages for assessment, \nattention should also be dedicated to three other considerations: \n• Text complexity \n• Text types \n• Text suitability for specific standards \nThese guidelines should inform the training of passage finders to ensure a pool of acceptable \npassages that can support assessment of all the Reading Informational Texts standards. They \nshould also alert form assemblers as they construct forms that will assess the complete range of \nskills. \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n120 \nAppendix D: Universal Design Item Checklist \nAppendix D: Universal Design Item Checklist \nUniversal Design Item Checklist \nA. Precisely Designed Constructs \nDefinition The item construct is clearly defined so that all irrelevant cognitive, \nemotional, and physical barriers are removed. \nsensory, \n√ The item does not add skills to those being measured (no extraneous skills tested). \nB. Language Appropriateness \nDefinition The item avoids words or phrases that are sexist, racist, or otherwise offensive, \ninappropriate, or negative to any subgroup. Language should be simple and clear. \n√ The item uses commonly used words—simpler is better. \n√ The item uses vocabulary appropriate for the grade. \n√ Idiomatic speech and figurative language are avoided unless being measured. \n√ The item avoids technical terms unrelated to the content. \n√ The item contains no unnecessary words. \n√ The sentence complexity contained in the item is appropriate for the grade. \n√ The item avoids ambiguous or multiple-meaning words (e.g., crane—the bird—can \neasily be confused with crane—heavy machinery). \n√ All pronouns have clear referents. \n√ The item avoids the use of proper names. (Such names \ndifficult for cultural subgroups.) \nmay be unfamiliar or \n√ The item avoids irregularly spelled words. \nC. Gender Stereotypes \nDefinition The item avoids stereotyping as results of associating genders with certain \nprofessions or activities. All groups of society should be portrayed accurately and \nfairly regarding gender. \n√ The item is free of content that might offend a gender subgroup. \n√ The item is \nsubgroup. \nfree of content that might unfairly advantage or disadvantage a gender \nD. Ethnic Stereotypes \nDefinition The item avoids unnecessary references to and uses the proper reference \nethnic, racial, or cultural groups. \nfor \n√ The item is free of content that might offend an ethnic subgroup. \n√ The item is \nsubgroup. \nfree of content that might unfairly advantage or disadvantage an ethnic \n√ The artwork \npopulation. \nincluded in an item adequately reflects the diversity of the student \nE. Cultural Familiarity \nDefinition Does not rely on an assumed shared experience that is class oriented or native\u0002English-speaking oriented. Presentations of cultural or ethnic differences should \nneither explicitly nor implicitly rely on stereotypes nor make moral judgments. \n√ The item does not rely on an assumed shared experience that is class oriented or \nnative-English-speaking oriented. \n√ The item is free from content that might offend a socioeconomic subgroup. \n√ The item is free of content that might unfairly advantage or disadvantage a \nsocioeconomic subgroup. \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n121 \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \nAppendix D: Universal Design Item Checklist \nUniversal Design Item Checklist \n√ The item is free from unnecessary cultural references. \n√ The item is free from religious references. \nF. Geographic Bias \nDefinition All groups of society should be portrayed accurately and fairly regarding \ngeographic setting. A particular geographic setting shouldn’t be used repeatedly, \nand urban, suburban, and rural settings should be represented across items. \n√ The item is free of content that might offend a geographic subgroup. \n√ The item is free of content that might unfairly advantage or disadvantage a \ngeographic subgroup. \nG. Disability Bias \nDefinition All groups of society should be portrayed accurately and fairly regarding disability. \nStereotypes related to any particular disability should be avoided. No undue \nrestrictions should exist in the item that would interfere with the ability of a student \nto comprehend or respond to the item. \n√ The item is free of content that might offend a disability subgroup. \n√ The item is free of content that might unfairly advantage or disadvantage a \ndisability subgroup. \n√ A graphic representation is used in the items, as appropriate. The complexity of the \ngraphic is appropriate to the purpose—simpler is better. \n√ The item avoids content that depends on sensory knowledge (such as references \nto movement, sound, smell, etc.) unless this is crucial to the overall item. \n√ The item could be put into braille. \n√ The item avoids using both O and Q. \n√ Letter pairs can be easily distinguished when read. (S and T are okay; S and X are \nnot). \nH. Art Supports Text \nDefinition The art is related to the item and supports the reader when possible. The item text \nand art are legible and accessible, and the art is appropriately placed in the item to \nsupport the reader. The art does not distract the test taker but instead provides a \nscaffold to overall comprehension. \n√ All pictures relate to items. \n√ The item is free from pictorial clutter: All pictures are needed to answer the item. \n√ Graphics are clear and non-fuzzy. \n√ Any symbols used are highly distinguishable. \n√ Visual load requirements are reasonable for the grade. \n√ Multi-dimensional graphics and complex shading are avoided. \n√ Tables have replaced any cluttered graphs. \n√ Labels read clockwise (as is easier for braille readers). \nI. Special Populations Considerations \nDefinition Consideration must be given for maximum accessibility to all students, including, \nbut not limited to, English Language Learners/Multilingual Learners, limited sight, \nhearing impaired, cognitively challenged, etc. These considerations will assist all \nstudents. \n√ The item contains scaffolding techniques to support student understanding of what \nis being asked in the item. \n√ Text is replaced with graphic representations, when appropriate. \n√ The item is written with simplified text load. \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n122 \n \n \n \nAppendix D: Universal Design Item Checklist \nUniversal Design Item Checklist \n√ The item is written with simplified sentences. \n√ The item has as little extraneous information as possible. \n√ The item provides context, but it is simplified. \n√ The item uses smaller or less-complicated numbers or expressions where not \notherwise required. \n√ The item avoids negative phrasing or questions; for example, questions are not \nasked in the negative. \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n123 \n \n \n \n \n \nAppendix E: Criteria for Item Acceptability \nAppendix E: Criteria for Item Acceptability \nThe following criteria represent best practices in item development and were implemented \nduring the creation and review of the New York State Grades 3–8 ELA and Mathematics Test \nitems. \nFor Multiple-Choice Items: \nCheck that the content of each item: \n• is targeted to assess only one objective or skill (unless specifications indicate otherwise) \n• deals with material that is important in testing the targeted performance indicator \n• uses grade-appropriate content and thinking skills \n• is presented at a reading level suitable for the grade being tested \n• has a stem that facilitates answering the question or completing the statement without \nlooking at the answer choices \n• has a stem that does not present clues to the correct answer choice \n• has answer choices that are plausible and attractive to the student who has not mastered \nthe objective or skill \n• has mutually exclusive distractors \n• has one and only one correct answer choice \n• is free of cultural, racial, ethnic, age, gender, disability, regional, or other apparent bias \nCheck that the format of each item: \n• is worded in the positive unless it is absolutely necessary to use the negative form \n• is free of extraneous words or expressions in both the stem and the answer choices \n(e.g., the same word or phrase does not begin each answer choice) \n• indicates emphasis on key words, such as “best,” “first,” “least,” “not,” and others that \nare important and might be overlooked \n• places the interrogative word at the beginning of a stem in the form of a question or \nplaces the omitted portion of an incomplete statement at the end of the statement \n• indicates the correct answer choice \n• provides the rationale for all distractors \n• is conceptually, grammatically, and syntactically consistent—between the stem and \nanswer choices and among the answer choices \n• has answer choices balanced in length or contains two long and two short answer choices \n• clearly identifies the passage or other stimulus material associated with the item \n• clearly identifies a need for art, if applicable, and the art is conceptualized and sketched, \nwith important considerations explicated \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n124 \n \n \n \n \n \nAppendix E: Criteria for Item Acceptability \nAlso check that: \n• one item does not present clues to the correct answer choice for any other item \n• any item based on a passage is answerable from the information given in the passage and \nis not dependent on skills related to other content areas \n• any item based on a passage is truly passage-dependent; that is, not answerable without \nreference to the passage \n• there is a balance of reasonable, non-stereotypical representation of economic classes, \nraces, cultures, ages, genders, and persons with disabilities in context and art \nFor Constructed-Response Items: \nCheck that the content of each item is: \n• designed to assess the targeted performance indicator \n• appropriate for the grade being tested \n• presented at a reading level suitable for the grade being tested \n• appropriate in context \n• written so that a student possessing the knowledge or skill being tested can construct a \nresponse that can be scored with the specified rubric or scoring tool; that is, the range of \npossible correct responses must be wide enough to allow for a diversity of responses but \nnarrow enough so that students who do not clearly show their grasp of the objective or \nskill being assessed cannot obtain the maximum score \n• presented without clues to the correct response \n• checked for accuracy and documented against reliable, up-to-date sources (including \nrubrics) \n• free of cultural, racial, ethnic, age, gender, disability, or other apparent bias \nCheck that the format of each item is: \n• appropriate for the question being asked and the intended response \n• worded clearly and concisely, using simple vocabulary and sentence structure \n• precise and unambiguous in its directions for the desired response \n• free of extraneous words or expressions \n• worded in the positive form rather than in the negative form \n• conceptually, grammatically, and syntactically consistent \n• marked with emphasis on key words, such as “best,” “first,” “least,” and others that are \nimportant and might be overlooked \n• clearly identified as needing art, if applicable, and the art is conceptualized and sketched, \nwith important considerations explicated \nAlso check that: \n• one item does not present clues to the correct response to any other item \n• there is a balance of reasonable, non-stereotypical representation of economic classes, \nraces, cultures, ages, genders, and persons with disabilities in context and art \n• for each set of items related to a reading passage, each item is designed to elicit a unique \nand independent response \n• items designed to assess reading do not depend on prior knowledge of the subject matter \nused in the prompt/question \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n125 \n \n \n \n \nAppendix F: Psychometric Guidelines for Operational Item Selection \nAppendix F: Psychometric Guidelines for Operational Item Selection \nIt is primarily up to the content-development department to select items for the 2024 operational \ntest. The psychometrics department provides support, as necessary, and reviews the final item \nselection. The psychometrics department provides data files with parameters for all FT items \neligible for the item pool. The pools of items eligible for 2024 item selection included 2019– \n2023 embedded and stand-alone field test items. \nHere are the general guidelines for item selection: \n• Satisfy the content specifications in terms of objective coverage and the number and \npercentage of MC and CR items on the test. An often-used criterion for objective \ncoverage is within 5% of the percentages of score points and items per objective. \n• To the extent possible, select both easy and difficult items to provide good measurement \ninformation at both ends of the performance scale. \n• Avoid selecting items with too high/low p values, items with flagged point-biserials, and \npoorly fitting items. \n• Minimize the number of items flagged for DIF (gender, ethnic, and High/Low Needs \nschools). Flagged items should be reviewed for content again. Keep in mind that some \nitems may be flagged for DIF by chance only and that their content may not necessarily \nbe biased against any of the analyzed subgroups. The psychometrics department provides \nDIF information for each item. It is also possible to get “significant” DIF but not bias if \nthe content is a necessary part of the construct that is measured; that is, there may be \nsome flagged DIF items that do not exhibit bias. \n• Provide NYSED with the following summary information: \no Overview of the statistical properties of the tests \no Blueprint comparison between the test build and the target—the focus is on the total \nnumber of points on the test \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n126 \n \n \nAppendix G: Operational Item Maps \nAppendix G: Operational Item Maps \nThe following tables show the operational item maps for the 2024 NYSTP Grades 3–8 ELA and Mathematics Tests. If the prompt requires two texts and the student only references one text, the response can be scored no higher \nthan a 1. Condition Code A is applied whenever a student who is present for a test session leaves an entire \nconstructed-response item in that session completely blank (no response attempted). \nCopyright © Condition Code A is applied whenever a student who is present for a test session leaves an entire constructed\u0002response item in that session completely blank (no response attempted). \n2-Credit Constructed-Response Holistic Rubric \n2 Credits \nA 2-credit response includes the correct solution to the question and demonstrates a \nthorough understanding of the mathematical concepts and/or procedures in the task. \nThis response: \n• indicates that the student has completed the task correctly, using \nmathematically sound procedures \n• contains sufficient work to demonstrate a thorough understanding of the \nmathematical concepts and/or procedures \n• may contain inconsequential errors that do not detract from the correct \nsolution and the demonstration of a thorough understanding \n1 Credit \nA 1-credit response demonstrates only a partial understanding of the mathematical \nconcepts and/or procedures in the task. \nThis response: \n• correctly addresses only some elements of the task \n• may contain an incorrect solution but applies a mathematically appropriate \nprocess \n• may contain the correct solution but required work is incomplete \n0 Credits \nA 0-credit response is incorrect, irrelevant, incoherent, or contains a correct solution \nobtained using an obviously incorrect procedure. Although some elements may \ncontain correct mathematical procedures, they are not holistically sufficient to \ndemonstrate even a limited understanding of the mathematical concepts embodied in \nthe task. \nNote. Condition Code A is applied whenever a student who is present for a test session leaves an entire constructed\u0002response item in that session completely blank (no response attempted). \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n150 \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \nAppendix K: Mathematics Extended-Response Rubric \nAppendix K: Mathematics Extended-Response Rubric \n3-Credit Constructed-Response Holistic Rubric \n3 Credits \nA 3-credit response includes the correct solution(s) to the question and demonstrates a \nthorough understanding of the mathematical concepts and/or procedures in the task. \nThis response: \n• indicates that the student has completed the task correctly, using \nmathematically sound procedures \n• contains sufficient work to demonstrate a thorough understanding of the \nmathematical concepts and/or procedures \n• may contain inconsequential errors that do not detract from the correct \nsolution(s) and the demonstration of a thorough understanding \n2 Credits \nA 2-credit response demonstrates a partial understanding of the mathematical \nconcepts and/or procedures in the task. \nThis response: \n• appropriately addresses most, but not all, aspects of the task using \nmathematically sound procedures \n• may contain an incorrect solution but provides sound procedures, reasoning, \nand/or explanations \n• may reflect some minor misunderstanding of the underlying mathematical \nconcepts and/or procedures \n1 Credit \nA 1-credit response demonstrates only a limited understanding of the mathematical \nconcepts and/or procedures in the task. \nThis response: \n• may address some elements of the task correctly but reaches an inadequate \nsolution and/or provides reasoning that is faulty or incomplete \n• exhibits multiple flaws related to misunderstanding of important aspects of \nthe task, misuse of mathematical procedures, or faulty mathematical \nreasoning \n• reflects a lack of essential understanding of the underlying mathematical \nconcepts \n• may contain the correct solution(s), but the required work is limited \n0 Credits \nA 0-credit response is incorrect, irrelevant, incoherent, or contains a correct solution \nobtained using an obviously incorrect procedure. Although some elements may \ncontain correct mathematical procedures, they are not holistically sufficient to \ndemonstrate even a limited understanding of the mathematical concepts embodied in \nthe task. \nNote. Condition Code A is applied whenever a student who is present for a test session leaves an entire constructed\u0002response item in that session completely blank (no response attempted). \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n151 \n \nAppendix L: Factor Analysis Results for Select Subgroups \nAppendix L: Factor Analysis Results for Selected Subgroups \nAs described in Section 3: Validity, a principal component factor analysis was conducted on the \n2024 Grades 3–8 ELA and Mathematics Tests data. It is assumed that examinees are classified \ninto one of H mutually exclusive categories on the basis of predetermined H - 1 observed score \ncutoffs, C1, C2, … , CH-1. Let Lh represent the h th category into which examinees with Ch−1 ≤ X < Ch \nare classified. C0 = 0 and CH = the maximum number-correct score plus one. Then, the \nconditional and marginal probabilities of each category classification are as follows: \nC −1 \nP(X ∈ Lh |θ ) = ∑\nh\nf (x |θ ) , h =1, 2,…, H x=Ch−1 \nC −1 \nP(X ∈ Lh ) = ∫ ∑\nh\nf (x |θ )g(θ )dθ , h =1, 2,…, H x=Ch−1 \nBecause obtaining test scores from two independent administrations of New York State tests was \nnot feasible due to item release after each operational (OP) administration, a psychometric model \nwas used to obtain the estimated classification consistency indices using test scores from a single \nadministration. Based on the psychometric model, a symmetric H-by-H contingency table can be \nconstructed. The elements of the H-by-H contingency table consist of the joint probabilities of \nthe row and column observed category classifications. \nThat two administrations are independent implies that if X1 and X2 represent the raw score \nrandom variables on the two administrations, then, conditioned on θ, X1 and X2 are independent \nand identically distributed. Consequently, the conditional bivariate distribution of X1 and X2 is \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n181 \nf (x1 , x2 |θ ) = f (x1 |θ ) f (x2 |θ ) \n \n \n \n \nAppendix O: Derivation and Estimation of Classification Consistency and Accuracy \nThe marginal bivariate distribution of X\nf (x1, x2 ) = ∫ f (x1, x2 |θ ) f (θ )dθ\n1 and X2 can be expressed as follows: \nConsistent classification means that both X1 and X2 fall in the same category. The conditional \nprobability of falling in the same category for the two administrations is \nCh−1 \nP(X1 ∈ Lh , X 2 ∈ Lh | θ ) = \n ∑ f (x1 | θ ) \n\n\n2\n, h =1, 2,…, H \nx1 =Ch −1 \nThe agreement index, P , conditional on theta, is obtained by \nH \nP(θ ) = ∑P(X1 ∈ Lh , X 2 ∈ Lh | θ ) \nh=1 \nThe agreement index (classification consistency) can be computed as \nP = ∫ P(θ )g(θ )d(θ ) \nThe probability of consistent classification by chance, PC , is the sum of squared marginal \nprobabilities of each category classification. \nH H 2\nPC = ∑P X( 1 ∈ L PX h ) ( 2 ∈ Lh ) = ∑[P X( 1 ∈ Lh )]\nh=1 h=1 \nThen, kappa (Cohen, 1960) is \nP − PC k = 1− PC \nClassification Accuracy \nLet Γw denote true category. When an examinee has an observed score, x L ∈ ( h = 1, 2, … , h \nH), and a latent score, θ ∈ Γ , (w = 1, 2, … , H) an accurate classification is made when h = w. w \nThe conditional probability of accurate classification is \nγ θ( ) = ( ∈ w PX L |θ ),\nWhere w is the category such that θ ∈Γw . \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n182 \n \n \n \n \nAppendix O: Derivation and Estimation of Classification Consistency and Accuracy \nLee (2010) thoroughly discusses this IRT method for estimating decision indices, including the \ncomputational method used to estimate the results when integrating across the latent variable, θ. \nEstimating Classification Indices \nThe classification consistency and accuracy estimates were obtained using an open-source \nsoftware program, IRT-CLASS v2.0 (Lee & Kolen, 2006). Below is a brief description of the \nfiles that are used and their purpose. (See the IRT-CLASS v2.0 manual for complete \ninstructions.) \nFiles needed: \n• Raw-to-scale score conversion file \na. Contains the raw-to-scale score conversions \nb. This is used to provide both raw and scale score classification estimates, which is \nuseful when the raw-to-scale score transformation is not one-to-one. \n• Cut score file \na. Contains the cut scores to be used \nb. Results are provided for all cut scores simultaneously (all performance levels), as \nwell as the estimates based on each of the cut scores separately (Level 3 only). \n• Item parameter file \na. This contains the IRT model used and item parameter estimates. \nb. This information is used when calculating the classification indices. \n• Theta file \na. Contains the theta distribution in terms of quadrature points \nb. The theta and the item parameter files are used to solve the integrals mentioned \nabove. \n• Control card \na. This is used to run the program. \nb. Identifies the names of the four files above and gives a name to the output file. \nCopyright © 2024 by the New York State Education Department \n183 \n \nAppendix P: Raw-Score-to-Scale-Score and Scale-Score Frequency Tables \nAppendix P: Raw-Score-to-Scale-Score and Scale-Score Frequency Tables \nTables P.1–P.12 show the raw-score-to-scale-score (RSSS) conversion tables.
